Priority of Maintenance over Debts in Bankruptcy
Ruling that maintenance of bankrupt and dependents takes precedence over creditors

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that the bankrupt's own maintenance and that of his dependents takes precedence over creditors' claims, and explain the two legal reasons (dalil aqli) for this priority.

Core Idea
The bankrupt's essential survival needs override creditors' rights until maintenance is secured.

Bankruptcy (hajr) does not strip a person of the basic right to maintain himself and his dependents. In Hanafi fiqh, the bankrupt's property is first used for his and his family's essential needs before creditors can claim anything. This lesson examines the ruling and its rational basis, including an interesting corollary about a new wife's dower.

Guided Path
1
Identify the recipients of maintenance
The passage lists: the bankrupt himself, his wife, his minor children, and his needy relatives (dhawu al-arham) whom he is obligated to support. Note that the bankrupt's own maintenance is also covered.
This includes blood relatives who are legally dependent on him.
2
Recognize the ruling's priority
The passage states that spending on these persons from the bankrupt's property is required even over the creditors' claims. The creditors do not have a prior claim until maintenance is provided.
3
Understand the first legal reason (dalil)
The first reason: 'his primary need is given precedence over the creditors' right'. This means the bankrupt's basic survival needs (food, clothing, shelter) are more essential than the creditors' financial rights.
This is a maqasid-based argument: preservation of life and family takes priority.
4
Understand the second legal reason
The second reason: 'it is a right established for others, so interdiction does not invalidate it'. The maintenance rights of dependents are established in the Shari'ah, and the bankrupt's interdiction (hajr) cannot cancel these rights that belong to others.
This shows that hajr only restricts the bankrupt's own disposal of property, not obligations toward dependents.
5
Apply the rule to the marriage example
If the bankrupt marries a woman, her proper dower (mahr mithl) becomes a debt like any other. The passage says she will share equally with creditors in the amount of her dower, meaning it is added to the list of debts after maintenance is deducted.
This example illustrates that new obligations arising during bankruptcy are also subject to the priority of maintenance over debts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و ينفق على المفلس من ماله وعلى زوجته وولده الصغار وذوى ارحامه ممن يجب نفقته عليه لان حاجته الاصلية مقدمة على حق الغرماء ولانه حق ثابت لغيره فلا يبطله الحجر ولهذا لو تزوج امرأة كانت في مقدار مهر مثلها اسوة للغرماء

ترجمہ: اور خرچ کیا جائے مفلس پر اس کے مال سے اور اس کی بیوی، چھوٹے بچوں اور اس کے ذوی الارحام پر جن کا نفقہ اس پر واجب ہے۔ کیونکہ اس کی اصل ضرورت حق غرماء پر مقدم ہے اور اس لئے کہ یہ حق ثابت ہے غیر کیلئے تو حجر اس کو باطل نہیں کرے گا۔ اس لئے اگر اس نے کسی عورت سے نکاح کر لیا تو وہ اپنے مہر مثل کی مقدار میں قرض خواہوں کی یکساں شریک ہوگی۔

تشریح: قوله و ينفق على المفلس .... الخ ۔ مفلس سے مراد وہی مدیوں مجبور ہے۔ یعنی مفلس اور اس کی بیوی اس کے چھوٹے
And spending is made on the bankrupt from his property, and on his wife, minor children, and his relatives whom he is obligated to support. Because his primary need is given precedence over the creditors' right, and because it is a right established for others, interdiction does not invalidate it. For this reason, if he married a woman, she will be an equal of the creditors in the amount of her mahr mithl (proper dower).

Translation: And spending is made on the bankrupt from his property, and on his wife, small children, and his relatives for whom maintenance is obligatory upon him. Because his essential need is preferred over the right of creditors, and because this is a right established for others, so interdiction does not invalidate it. Therefore, if he married a woman, she will share equally with the creditors in the amount of her proper dower.

Commentary: His saying: 'and spending is made on the bankrupt...' etc. By 'bankrupt' is meant the same debtor who is under interdiction. That is, the bankrupt and his wife and his small...
Source pages p. 289

Commentary
This passage states that the bankrupt's own maintenance and that of his dependents (wife, minor children, and relatives he must support) are to be taken from his property before satisfying creditors. The reason is that his primary need (essential survival) takes precedence over the creditors' claims. Additionally, these maintenance rights are established for the dependents and cannot be invalidated by interdiction. An example is given: if the bankrupt marries a woman, she receives her proper dower (mahr mithl) and becomes one of the creditors, sharing equally with others, because her right is like any other debt. The commentary notes that this is based on the Sahibayn's view.

Key Terms
نفقہ (nafaqah): Maintenance; spending on food, clothing, shelter, and other necessities for oneself and dependents.
غرماء (ghurama): Creditors; those to whom the bankrupt owes debts.
مہر مثل (mahr mithl): Proper dower; the amount of dower customary for a woman of similar status.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is the bankrupt's own maintenance given priority over creditors' claims?
A) Because the creditors agreed to it.
B) Because his essential need is superior to their right.
C) Because the bankrupt can pay later.
D) Because the creditors are less important.

Key Takeaway
The bankrupt's and his dependents' maintenance is an obligatory expense that trumps creditors' claims, grounded in the Shari'ah's prioritization of essential needs.

Imprisonment and Treatment of the Bankrupt Debtor
Rulings on imprisoning bankrupt, proof, illness, and work

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rulings on imprisoning a bankrupt debtor, the conditions for his release, and the restrictions on his work in prison.

Core Idea
A bankrupt is imprisoned for contractual debts but released if he proves insolvency; in prison, he is not allowed to work to pressure him to pay.

This passage from Al-Hidayah details the procedures for handling a bankrupt debtor who claims to have no property. The Hanafi school balances the rights of creditors with the debtor's well-being, allowing imprisonment but with important releases for proof of insolvency or illness, and restricting work to exert pressure. The rulings are based on Quranic principles of respite and on analogical reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Determine what is being ruled upon: imprisonment of the bankrupt when creditors demand and he claims no property. The judge is commanded to imprison him.
The debt must be undertaken by contract (e.g., dower, surety).
2
Recognize the exception for proof of insolvency
If the bankrupt establishes evidence (bayyinah) that he has no property, he is released due to the obligation of granting respite (nazarah ila al-maisarah) based on Quran 2:280.
Bayyinah can be witnesses or documents.
3
Examine the illness rule
If he falls ill in prison and has a servant to care for him, he stays; otherwise, he is released to prevent destruction (danger to life).
This prioritizes preservation of life over detention.
4
Determine the work restriction for a professional
A professional craftsman (muhtarif) is not allowed to work in prison – this is the correct view (huwa al-sahih) – so that his heart becomes distressed and he is motivated to repay.
The restriction is coercive, not punitive.
5
Distinguish the case of a slave-girl
If the debtor has a slave-girl and a private place, he is not prevented from intercourse, because fulfilling one desire (sexual) is analogized to allowing another (food/eating).
This is an example of qiyas khafi (hidden analogy).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال فان لم يعرف للمفلس مال وطلب غرماء حبسه وهو يقول لا مال لي حبسه الحاكم في كل دين التزمه بعقد كالمهر والكفالة وقد ذكرنا هذا الفصل بوجوهه في كتاب ادب القاضى من هذا الكتاب فلا نعيدها الى ان قال وكذلك ان اقام البينة انه لا مال له يعنى خلى سبيله لوجوب النظرة الى الميسرة ولو مرض فى الحبس يبقى فيه ان كان له خادم يقوم بمعالجته وان لم يكن اخرجه تحرزا عن هلاكه والمحترف فيه لا يُمكن من الاشتغال بعمله هو الصحيح ليضجر قلبه فينبعث على قضاء دينه بخلاف ما اذا كانت له جارية وفيه موضع يمكنه فيه وطيها لا يمنع عنه لانه قضاء احدى الشهوتين فيعتبر بقضاء الأخرى

ترجمہ: پھر اگر معلوم نہ ہوتا ہو مفلس کا کوئی مال اور طلب کریں قرض خواہ اس کو قید کرنا اور وہ یہی کہے کہ میرے پاس مال نہیں ہے تو حاکم اس کو قید کر لے۔ ہر ایسے دین میں جس کا التزام کیا ہو اس نے عقد کے ذریعہ سے جیسے مہر اور کفالت اور ہم نے اس فصل کو اس کی وجوہ کے ساتھ کتاب ادب القاضی میں ذکر کیا ہے تو اس کا اعادہ نہیں کریں گے اس قول تک کہ ایسے ہی اگر اس نے بینہ قائم کر دیا کہ اس کے پاس کچھ مال نہیں ہے یعنی اسکی راہ چھوڑ دے آسمانی کے وقت تک وجوب مہلت کی وجہ سے اور اگر وہ قید خانہ میں بیمار ہو گیا تو اس کو وہیں چھوڑ دیا جائیگا۔ اگر اس کا کوئی خادم ہو جو اسکے معالجہ کی پرداخت کر سکے اور اگر نہ ہو تو اس کو نکال دے اس کی ہلاکت سے بچنے کیلئے اور پیشہ ور کو اپنے کام میں مشغول ہونے کا قابو نہیں دیا جائے گا۔ یہی صحیح ہے تا کہ وہ تنگ اور زچ ہو کر بعجلت اداء قرض پر آمادہ ہو۔ بخلاف اس کے جب مدیون کی کوئی باندی ہو اور قید خانہ میں کوئی ایسی جگہ ہو جہاں وہ وطی کر سکتا ہے کہ اس سے منع نہیں کیا جائے گا۔ کیونکہ یہ دو شہوتوں میں سے ایک کو پورا کرنا ہے تو اس کو دوسری شہوت پر قیاس کیا جائے گا۔
If no property of the bankrupt is known and the creditors demand his imprisonment, and he says 'I have no property', the judge shall imprison him. For every debt he has undertaken by contract, like dower and surety. We have already mentioned this chapter with its aspects in Kitab Adab al-Qadi from this book, so we will not repeat it, until he said: and similarly if he establishes evidence that he has no property, meaning he is released because of the obligation of granting respite until ease. And if he becomes ill in prison, he remains there if he has a servant to treat him; if not, he is released to avoid his destruction. And the professional (craftsman) is not allowed to engage in his work – that is the correct view – so that his heart becomes distressed and he is motivated to repay his debt. Unlike the case where he has a slave-girl and there is a place in the prison where he can have intercourse with her; he is not prevented from that, because it is fulfilling one of two desires, so it is analogized to the other desire.
Source pages p. 289

Commentary
This passage details the procedures for a bankrupt who claims to have no property. If creditors demand imprisonment and he asserts poverty, the judge may imprison him for any debt undertaken by contract (like dower or surety). The author notes that the full discussion is in Kitab Adab al-Qadi. If the bankrupt provides evidence (bayyinah) of insolvency, he is released due to the obligation of granting respite (nazarah ila al-maisarah). Regarding illness: if he has a servant to care for him, he stays in prison; otherwise, he is released to prevent death. A professional craftsman is not allowed to work in prison, so that pressure motivates him to pay his debt. However, if he has a slave-girl and a private place in prison, he is not prevented from intercourse, as fulfilling one desire is analogized to allowing another (eating).

Key Terms
بینہ (bayyinah): Evidence; proof of claims, typically through witnesses or documents.
نظرۃ الی المیسرة (nazarah ila al-maisarah): Respite until ease; granting a debtor time until he is able to pay.
محترف (muhtarif): Professional; a craftsman or skilled worker.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling on a professional craftsman imprisoned for debt regarding his work?
A) He must be allowed to work to earn money for his debt.
B) He is not allowed to work to increase pressure on him.
C) He is allowed to work only if he promises to pay.
D) He is allowed to work but not outside prison.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school uses imprisonment as a coercive measure for contractual debts but prioritizes the debtor's life and permits release upon proof of insolvency or illness without care, while prohibiting work to increase pressure to pay.

Spending on the Bankrupt Debtor and His Dependents
Explanation of the ruling on spending from bankrupt's wealth on himself and dependents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on spending on a bankrupt debtor and his dependents, explain the reasoning based on priority of primary need and that the right is established for others, and apply the rule to the wife's mahr.

Core Idea
Maintenance of self and dependents takes priority over creditors even for a bankrupt debtor.

In this passage from Al-Hidayah, we learn that a debtor placed under interdiction (hajr) is still obligated to provide for himself and his dependents from his own wealth before paying creditors. The commentator explains the ruling and its rationale, and extends it to the wife's mahr as a similar right.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and their rights
Recognize the debtor, his dependents (wife, young children, relatives), and the creditors. Understand that interdiction restricts the debtor's disposition but does not eliminate pre-existing rights.
2
State the ruling
The ruling is that maintenance of the bankrupt and his dependents is paid from his wealth before creditors. This includes himself, his wife, young children, and relatives whose support is wajib.
3
Analyse the first proof: priority of primary need
The first reason given is that the debtor's basic need (hajah asliyyah) takes precedence over the creditors' right. This is a principle of necessity overriding financial claims.
4
Analyse the second proof: right established for others
The second reason is that the right of maintenance is established for the dependents (haqq thabit li ghayrihi), and interdiction only restricts the debtor's own actions, not rights that belong to others.
5
Apply to the wife's mahr
The same principle applies: if the debtor marries, the wife's mahr mithl is treated like any other debt – she becomes a creditor and shares proportionately with others up to that amount.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مفلس کے مال سے مفلس اور اس کے اہل وعیال پر خرچ کیا جائے و ينفق على المفلس من ماله وعلى زوجته وولده الصغار وذوى ارحامه ممن يجب نفقته عليه لان حاجته الاصلية مقدمة على حق الغرماء ولانه حق ثابت لغيره فلا يبطله الحجر ولهذا لو تزوج امرأة كانت في مقدار مهر مثلها اسوة للغرماء ترجمہ اور خرچ کیا جائے مفلس پر اس کے مال سے اور اس کی بیوی ، چھوٹے بچوں اور اس کے ذوی الارحام پر جن کا نفقہ اس پر واجب ہے۔ کیونکہ اس کی اصل ضرورت حق غرماء پر مقدم ہے اور اس لئے کہ یہ حق ثابت ے غیر کیلئے تو حجر اس کو باطل نہیں کرے گا۔ اس لئے اگر اس نے کسی عورت سے نکاح کر لیا تو وہ اپنے مہر مثل کی مقدار میں قرض خواہوں کی یکساں شریک ہوگی ۔ تشریح... قوله و ينفق على المفلس .... الخ ۔ مفلس سے مراد وہی مدیوں مجبور ہے۔ یعنی مفلس اور اس کی بیوی اس کے چھوٹے بچے اور دور الارحام کا نفقہ اس کے مال سے دیا جائے گا۔ کیونکہ اس کی اصلی ضرورت حق غرماء پر مقدم ہے۔ نیز اسلئے بھی کہ نفقہ کا حق تو دوسروں کیلئے ثابت ہے تو حجر اس کو باطل نہیں کر سکتا۔ اور اسی وجہ سے کہ اس کی حالت اصلیہ حق غرماء پر مقدم ہے۔ اگر اس نے کسی عورت سے نکاح کر لیا تو وہ اپنے مہر مثل کی مقدار تک قرض خواہوں کی یکساں شریک ہوگی۔
Spend on the bankrupt from his wealth and on his wife, young children, and relatives whose maintenance is obligatory upon him, because his primary need takes precedence over the right of creditors, and because it is a right established for others, so interdiction does not invalidate it. Hence, if he marries a woman, she is like the creditors in the amount of her mahr mithl. Translation: Spend on the bankrupt from his wealth and on his wife, young children, and relatives whose maintenance is obligatory upon him. Because his basic need takes precedence over the right of creditors, and because this right is established for others, interdiction does not invalidate it. Therefore, if he married a woman, she will share equally with the creditors in the amount of her proper dowry. Explanation: His saying 'and he spends on the bankrupt...' etc. The bankrupt means the same indebted person. That is, the maintenance of the bankrupt and his wife, his young children, and distant relatives will be given from his wealth. Because his basic need takes precedence over the right of creditors. Also because the right of maintenance is established for others, so interdiction cannot invalidate it. And for this reason, since his original state takes precedence over the right of creditors, if he married a woman, she will share equally with the creditors up to the amount of her proper dowry.
Source pages p. 289

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on Al-Hidayah explains the ruling that a bankrupt debtor's essential living expenses and those of his dependents are paid from his own wealth before satisfying creditors. The reasoning is that his primary need (hajah asliyyah) takes priority over the claims of creditors. The maintenance of his wife, children, and relatives whose support is obligatory upon him is considered a right established for these dependents (haqq thabit li ghayrihi), and interdiction (hajr) does not invalidate this right. Similarly, if the debtor marries, his wife's mahr (dower) is treated like any other debt; she becomes a creditor and shares proportionately with other creditors to the amount of her proper mahr (mahr mithl). This is based on the principle that the debtor's essential need and the rights of others that are not created by the interdiction itself remain valid.

Key Terms
حجر (hajr): Interdiction or legal restriction placed on a debtor who is declared bankrupt, preventing him from disposing of his property.
نفقة (nafaqah): Maintenance and financial support for wife, children, and relatives.
مهر مثل (mahr mithl): The proper dowry equivalent to that of her peers, used when no specific mahr is fixed.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this passage, if a bankrupt debtor marries, how is the wife's mahr treated?
A) It is paid in full before any creditors.
B) She shares proportionately with other creditors up to the amount of her mahr mithl.
C) It is cancelled due to the interdiction.
D) The husband does not have to pay it.

Key Takeaway
The primary need of a bankrupt debtor and his dependents takes precedence over creditors' claims, and interdiction does not invalidate rights established for others.

Sick Debtor in Prison and Work Prohibition
Rulings on sick and working debtors in prison

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions for releasing a sick debtor from prison and explain the Hanafi ruling on prohibiting a professional debtor from working, including the qiyas for allowing intercourse with a slave-girl.

Core Idea
A debtor's life takes priority over creditors' rights; work prohibition pressures payment, but natural desires are not prevented.

This passage from Al-Hidayah addresses two situations concerning imprisoned debtors. First, if the debtor falls ill, his release depends on whether he has a caretaker. Second, a debtor who is a professional (craftsman) is not allowed to work while in prison – this is the correct view – but if he owns a slave-girl and has a private place, he may have intercourse with her. The reason for the work prohibition is to psychologically pressure the debtor to pay, while the allowance for sexual intercourse is analogized to eating, as both fulfill natural desires.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two rulings
Read the passage and identify the two distinct legal rulings: (1) the condition for releasing a sick debtor, and (2) the prohibition of work for a professional debtor, with an exception for intercourse with a slave-girl.
The first ruling is about preserving life; the second is about pressuring the debtor to pay.
2
Analyze the condition for release
Note that the debtor remains in prison if he has a servant (khadim) who can treat him. If he has no such servant, he is released to prevent death. This shows that the preservation of life overrides the rights of creditors.
The servant must be capable of providing treatment.
3
Examine the work prohibition
The text states that a professional (muhtarij) is not allowed to work while imprisoned. The purpose is to distress him and motivate him to repay his debt. This is the correct view (huwa al-sahih).
Work would ease his mind and defeat the purpose of imprisonment.
4
Understand the qiyas for the slave-girl exception
If the debtor has a slave-girl and a place to have intercourse, he is not prevented. This is because fulfilling sexual desire is a natural need, analogous to eating. The text says it is 'qada' ihda al-shahwatayn' (fulfilling one of the two desires) and is analogized to the other desire (eating).
The qiyas is jaliy (clear) because the illa (effective cause) is the fulfillment of a natural desire.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله ولو مرض فى الحبس يبقى فيه ان كان له خادم يقوم بمعالجته وان لم يكن اخرجه تحرزا عن هلاكه والمحترف فيه لا يُمكن من الاشتغال بعمله هو الصحيح ليضجر قلبه فينبعث على قضاء دينه بخلاف ما اذا كانت له جارية وفيه موضع يمكنه فيه وطيها لا يمنع عنه لانه قضاء احدى الشهوتين فيعتبر بقضاء الأخرى قوله ولو مرض فى الحبس ... الخ اگر مفلس مذکور قید خانہ میں بیمار ہو گیا تو وہیں چھوڑ دیا جائے گا۔ بشرطیکہ اس کا ایسا کوئی مذکور خادم ہو جو ا سکے علاج و معالجہ کی پرداخت کر سکے اور اگر کوئی ایسا خادم نہ ہوتو قید خانہ سے نکال دیا جائے گا تا کہ وہ ہلاک نہ ہو جائے ۔ کیونکہ
His saying: 'And if he falls ill in prison, he remains there if he has a servant to treat him; if not, he is released to prevent his destruction. And the professional (craftsman) is not allowed to engage in his work; this is the correct view, so that his heart becomes distressed and he is prompted to repay his debt. Unlike the case if he has a slave-girl and there is a place where he can have intercourse with her; he is not prevented, because it is fulfilling one of the two desires, so it is analogized to fulfilling the other.' Commentary: 'His saying: And if he falls ill in prison... etc. If the bankrupt falls ill in prison, he is left there, provided he has a servant who can undertake his treatment. If he has no such servant, he is released from prison to prevent his destruction, because...'
Source pages p. 290

Commentary
This passage addresses two situations: (1) A debtor imprisoned for debt who falls ill. If he has a servant to care for him, he stays in prison. If not, he is released to prevent his death. This is based on preserving life, which takes priority over the creditors' rights. (2) A debtor who is a professional (craftsman) is not allowed to work while imprisoned. This is the correct view (huwa al-sahih) because the purpose of imprisonment is to pressure him psychologically so that he becomes distressed and motivated to pay his debt. Allowing him to work would ease his mind and defeat the purpose. However, if he has a slave-girl and there is a suitable place in prison, he is allowed to have intercourse with her. This is because satisfying sexual desire is considered a natural need, analogous to eating and drinking. The text says it is 'fulfilling one of the two desires' (qada' ihda al-shahwatayn) and is analogized to the other (i.e., eating). Thus, it is not prevented.

Key Terms
خادم (khadim): A male or female servant who can care for the debtor.
محترف (muhtarij): A person who earns his livelihood by a craft or profession.
جارية (jariyah): A female slave owned by the debtor.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the purpose of prohibiting a professional debtor from working in prison according to this passage?
A) To prevent him from earning too much money.
B) To distress his heart and prompt him to repay his debt.
C) To punish him for being a professional.
D) To avoid unfair competition with free workers.

Key Takeaway
Hanafi law balances creditor rights with debtor welfare: release a sick debtor without caretaker to save life, but deny work to professionals to apply pressure, while natural needs like intercourse are not restricted.

Permissibility for a Debtor to Have Intercourse with His Slave-girl
Ruling that a debtor is not prevented from intercourse with his slave-girl

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that a debtor under hajr is not prevented from intercourse with his own slave-girl, and explain the qiyas used to justify it.

Core Idea
Basic human needs are not overridden by debt restrictions.

This lesson examines a specific Hanafi ruling regarding a debtor in prison who owns a slave-girl. The text establishes that he is not prevented from having intercourse with her, drawing an analogy with the natural need to eat. We will unpack the logical structure of this ruling and its underlying principle.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act in question is a debtor having intercourse with his own slave-girl while imprisoned. The ruling is that he is not prevented (mubah).
2
Note the condition
The ruling is contingent on the existence of a suitable place in prison where he can have intercourse privately.
If no such place exists, the ruling may differ.
3
Understand the restriction (hajr)
Hajr (legal restriction) is imposed on a debtor to protect creditors' rights, but it does not prevent basic personal needs.
The restriction primarily affects financial transactions.
4
Analyze the qiyas
The ruling is justified by qiyas: asl (eating food to satisfy hunger) is permitted; far' (intercourse to satisfy sexual desire) shares the illa of natural desire; thus both are not prevented.
The illa is shahwah (natural desire), not survival necessity.
5
Conclude the ruling
Therefore, the debtor may have intercourse with his slave-girl without hindrance, provided conditions are met.

Source Text

This passage differentiates the case of a debtor who owns a slave-girl. If the prison has a suitable place, he is not prevented from having intercourse with her. The analogy (qiyas) is with the need to eat: just as a debtor is not prevented from eating to satisfy hunger, he is not prevented from sexual intercourse to satisfy natural desire. This shows that basic human needs are not overridden by the restriction of hajr.

Qadi's Non-Intervention and Creditors' Rights after Bankruptcy: Abu Hanifa vs. Sahibayn
Intra-Hanafi dispute on qadi's intervention and creditors' mulazama after release.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the difference between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on the effect of iflas on mulazama, and apply the ruling to a scenario.

Core Idea
A judge's bankruptcy declaration does not automatically extinguish creditors' right to pursue the debtor.

This passage from al-Hidayah presents an internal Hanafi dispute over the legal effect of a bankruptcy declaration after a debtor's release from prison. Abu Hanifa holds that the declaration does not establish true financial hardship because wealth fluctuates, so creditors retain the right of mulazama (following). The Sahibayn argue that the declaration is valid and grants the debtor respite unless creditors prove hidden assets.

Guided Path
1
Identify the central question
What happens to creditors' rights (mulazama and taqadi) after the judge declares a debtor bankrupt and releases him from prison? Does the judge intervene to stop creditors from pursuing the debtor?
2
State Abu Hanifa's position and his dalil
Abu Hanifa says the qadi does not intervene; creditors can follow and demand payment. His evidence is the hadith 'لصاحب الحق يد ولسان' and the reasoning that bankruptcy is not conclusively proven (wealth can appear/disappear).
3
State the Sahibayn's position and their dalil
The Sahibayn say the qadi does intervene once he declares iflas, because the declaration establishes 'usr (hardship) and entitles the debtor to respite. Creditors can only pursue if they bring proof of hidden assets.
4
Analyse the thamarah al-khilaf (practical difference)
Under Abu Hanifa, creditors can physically accompany the debtor everywhere and take surplus earnings. Under the Sahibayn, creditors cannot follow unless they prove the debtor has money; the judge grants a respite.
The tarjih in the matn favors Abu Hanifa, but later fatwa often follows the Sahibayn.
5
Apply the ruling to a modern scenario
Consider a debtor released from prison after a bankruptcy order. Under which view can the creditor still wait outside his workplace to collect installment payments?
This helps internalize the difference.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال ولا يحول بينه وبين غرمائه بعد خروجه من الحبس بل يلازمونه ولا يمنعونه من التصرف والسفر لقوله عليه السلام لصاحب الحق يد ولسان اراد باليد الملازمة وباللسان التقاضي قال وياخذون فضل كسبه يقسم بينهم بالحصص لاستواء حقوقهم فى القوة وقالا اذا فلسه الحاكم حال بين الغرماء وبينه الا ان يقيموا البينة ان له مالا لان القضاء بالافلاس عندهما يصح فيثبت العسرة ويستحق النظرة الى الميسرة وعند ابي حنيفة لا يتحقق القضاء بالافلاس لان مال الله تعالى عادٍ ورائح ولان وقوف الشهود على عدم المال لا يتحقق الاظاهرا فيصلح للدفع لا لابطال حق الملازمة وقوله الا ان يقيموا البيئة اشارة الى ان بيئة اليسار تترجح على بينة الاعسار لانها اكثر اثباتا اذ الاصل هو العسرة وقوله فى الملازمة لا يمنعونه من التصرف والسفر دليل على انه يدور معه اينما دار ولا يجلسه في موضع لانه حبس فيه
He said: He does not intervene between him and his creditors after his release from prison, but they follow him (mulazama) and they do not prevent him from disposing of property and traveling, because of his saying, peace be upon him: 'For the right-holder is the hand and the tongue.' He intended by 'hand' the following (mulazama) and by 'tongue' the demanding (taqadi). He said: And they take the surplus of his earnings, dividing it among themselves proportionally because of the equality of their rights in strength. And they (the two companions) said: When the judge declares him bankrupt (iflas), he (the judge) intervenes between the creditors and him, unless they (creditors) establish proof (bayyinah) that he has property. Because, according to them (the Sahibayn), the judgment of bankruptcy (iflas) is valid, so hardship ('usr) is established and he becomes entitled to respite until ease (yusr). According to Abu Hanifa, the judgment of bankruptcy is not realized because the wealth of Allah (subhan) comes and goes, and because the witnesses' knowledge of the lack of wealth is not realized except apparently, so it is suitable for repelling (daf') but not for invalidating the right of mulazama. And his saying 'unless they establish proof' indicates that proof of solvency (yasar) outweighs proof of insolvency, because it is more affirmative, since the original state is scarcity. And his saying concerning mulazama: 'They do not prevent him from disposal and travel' is evidence that he (the creditor) goes around with him wherever he goes, and does not make him sit in one place, because that would be imprisonment.
Source pages p. 291

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute (Abu Hanifa vs. the Sahibayn) regarding the legal consequences of a qadi declaring a debtor bankrupt (muflis) after his release from prison. The matn of al-Hidayah states that the qadi does not intervene; rather, creditors have the right of mulazama (physically following the debtor) and taqadi (verbal demand). They can also take his surplus earnings pro rata. The Sahibayn argue that once the qadi declares bankruptcy, he intervenes to prevent creditors from pursuing him unless they prove hidden assets. Their reasoning: the judgment of iflas is valid, confirming hardship and entitling the debtor to respite. Abu Hanifa (followed by the author) holds that bankruptcy is not legally established because wealth fluctuates and witnesses cannot know of its absence with certainty; thus the declaration serves only to repel immediate claims, not to abolish mulazama. The balance of proof favors solvency since the default is scarcity. The text also notes that the creditor's right to follow does not include imprisoning the debtor in a specific place. A later tanbih clarifies that the hadith (لصاحب الحق يد ولسان) is not 'mutlaq' regarding time but 'sakit'; the correct reasoning is that the 'illa' (effective cause) of being a right-holder exists both before and after imprisonment, so the ruling applies equally. The fa'idah adds a supporting hadith from Abu Dawood.

Key Terms
ملازمة (mulazama): The right of a creditor to physically accompany the debtor to pressure him into payment.
تقاضي (taqadi): The right of a creditor to verbally demand payment from the debtor.
افلاس (iflas): A judicial declaration of bankruptcy, establishing the debtor's financial incapability.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Sahibayn, what must creditors do to regain the right of mulazama after a debtor is declared bankrupt?
A) Wait until the debtor's situation improves.
B) Obtain a new court order.
C) Prove that the debtor possesses hidden assets.
D) The right is lost permanently.

Key Takeaway
The intra-Hanafi dispute on iflas shows that a judicial declaration does not universally override creditors' rights; the epistemological force of the declaration determines whether mulazama survives.

Validity of Bankruptcy Declaration: Abu Hanifa vs. Sahibayn
Commentary on the validity of declaring bankruptcy and the difference between Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on the validity of a judge's bankruptcy declaration, explain the reasoning behind each position, and identify the mufta bihi (operative ruling) in the Hanafi school.

Core Idea
A judge's bankruptcy declaration is valid according to the Sahibayn and Three Imams, but Imam Abu Hanifa rejects it due to wealth's transience.

This passage from al-Hidayah addresses an intra-Hanafi dispute: can a judge formally declare a debtor bankrupt? Imam Abu Hanifa argues that such a declaration is inherently uncertain and therefore invalid, while his two main disciples, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani (the Sahibayn), along with the Three Imams, hold that it is valid and provides the debtor with a grace period. The commentary also clarifies that when testimonies conflict, evidence of solvency takes precedence over evidence of insolvency.

Guided Path
1
Identify the contested issue
What exactly is being disputed? Is it the permissibility of declaring bankruptcy, or the validity of a judge's formal verdict? The dispute centers on 'qada' bi al-iflas' — the judge's ruling that a person is bankrupt.
The issue is not about whether bankruptcy exists, but about the legal effect of a judicial declaration.
2
State the position of the Sahibayn (and Three Imams)
The Sahibayn hold that the judge's declaration of bankruptcy is valid. Once declared, the debtor's insolvency is established, and he becomes entitled to a moratorium (grace period) until he acquires wealth. The Three Imams (Malik, Shafi'i, Ahmad) agree.
The Sahibayn rely on practical necessity: creditors need a formal mechanism to protect their rights.
3
State the position of Imam Abu Hanifa and his proof
Imam Abu Hanifa rejects the validity of a formal bankruptcy declaration. His proof is rational (dalil aqli): wealth is transient — a person may be wealthy today and poor tomorrow, so a judgment based on present insolvency is unreliable. Additionally, witnesses cannot have certain knowledge of another's lack of wealth; they only see outward appearances. Therefore, such testimony cannot definitively establish insolvency, and the creditor's right to pursue the debt remains intact.
Abu Hanifa cites a poetic verse by Hatim: 'Do you not see that wealth goes and comes, and only hadiths and remembrance remain from wealth?'
4
Understand the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
Under the Sahibayn's view, if a judge declares a debtor bankrupt, the debtor receives a grace period (in al-Hidayah referred to as 'mahl') until he becomes wealthy again. Under Abu Hanifa's view, no formal declaration is made, so the creditor can continue to demand payment immediately and the judge cannot force the creditor to wait.
The practical difference affects the rights of both creditor and debtor in matters of debt collection.
5
Recognize the mufta bihi and additional ruling on testimony
The relied-upon position for fatwa (mufta bihi) is that of the Sahibayn, as indicated by al-Marghinani's preference. Moreover, the commentary clarifies al-Quduri's wording: if there is conflicting testimony about solvency and insolvency, the testimony of solvency takes precedence. Reason: insolvency is the norm (asl), and witnesses of insolvency only confirm the norm, while witnesses of solvency prove an exceptional state.
This rule of testimony precedence is a secondary but important derivation from the dispute.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الحجر
۲۹۲
اشرف البدایہ شرح اردو ہدایہ جلد ۱۲
کے درمیان حائل ہو جائے اور ہمہ وقت تقاضا نہ کرنے دے الا یہ کہ فر ما ، اس پر بعینہ قائم کر دیں کہ اس کی ملک میں کچھ مال ہے۔ وجہ یہ ہے کہ قاضی کا کسی کے حق میں افلاس کا فیصلہ کرنا صاحبین کے نزدیک صحیح ہوتا ہے۔ پس مفلس کی عسرت و ناداری ثابت ہوگئی اور وہ مالداری
تک مہات دئیے جانے کا مستحق ہو گیا۔ ائمہ ثلاثہ بھی اس کے قائل ہیں اور امام ابوحنیفہ کے نزدیک قضاء بالافلاس بیچ نہیں ہوتی ۔ کیونکہ مال تو آنی جانی چیز ہے آج ہے کل نہیں۔ کل ہے پرسوں نہیں ، ولنعم ما قال حاتم
اما وى أن المال غاد ورائح و يبقى من المال الاحاديث والذكر نیز اس لئے بھی کہ مال نہ ہونے پر گواہوں کی واقفیت متحقق نہیں ہو سکتی کیونکہ ان کو حقیقت حال پر آگاہی نہیں ہے وہ تو صرف ظاہری طور پر یہ دریافت کر سکتے ہیں کہ ان کا مال نہیں ہے تو اس گواہی سے صرف دفعیہ ہو سکتا ہے فر ما ء کا حق ملازمت باطل نہیں ہو سکتا۔ قوله وقوله الا ان يقيمو الخ یعنی امام قدوری نے جو قول صاحبین کے ذیل میں کہا ہے الا ان یقیمو البینہ ادھ، اس میں اس بات کی طرف اشارہ ہے کہ مفلسی کی گواہی پر آسودگی کی گواہی کو ترجیح ہوگی کیونکہ تنگدستی اصل ہے اور تنگدستی کے گواہوں نے میں اصل ثابت کی ہے اور آسودگی کے گواہوں نے اس سے زیادہ یعنی آسودہ ہونا ثابت کیا ہے۔
The Book of Hajr. 292. Ashraf al-Bidayah Sharh Urdu Hidayah Volume 12. ... becomes an obstacle between them and does not allow constant demand, unless they (the creditors) establish evidence that he has some property in his possession. The reason is that the qadi's judgment of bankruptcy in favor of someone is valid according to the Sahibayn. Thus the insolvency and poverty of the bankrupt is proven, and he becomes entitled to a moratorium until wealth. The Three Imams also hold this view. And according to Imam Abu Hanifa, a judgment of bankruptcy is not valid because wealth is a transient thing: today it exists, tomorrow it does not; yesterday it existed, the day after tomorrow it does not. And how excellent is what Hatim said: 'Do you not see that wealth goes and comes, and only hadiths and remembrance remain from wealth?' Also because witnesses cannot have verified knowledge of the absence of wealth, since they are not aware of the reality; they can only ascertain apparently that he has no wealth, so this testimony can only be defensive; the creditor's right of pursuit cannot be invalidated. His statement (al-Quduri's) 'and his statement except if they establish evidence' indicates that testimony of solvency takes precedence over testimony of insolvency, because insolvency is the norm and the witnesses of insolvency have established the norm, while the witnesses of solvency have established something additional, i.e., being affluent.
Source pages p. 293

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on a ruling about declaring bankruptcy. It presents an intra-Hanafi dispute: the Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) hold that a judge can issue a formal judgment of bankruptcy, thereby confirming the debtor's insolvency and granting him a grace period until he becomes wealthy. This view is also held by the Three Imams (Imam Malik, al-Shafi'i, Ahmad). However, Imam Abu Hanifa rejects the validity of such a judgment. His evidence is rational: wealth is inherently transient—a person may be wealthy today and poor tomorrow, so a judgment based on present insolvency is unreliable. He cites the poet Hatim's saying that wealth comes and goes, but only lasting things remain. Additionally, witnesses cannot truly know whether someone has no wealth; they only observe outward circumstances, so their testimony cannot definitively establish insolvency. Therefore, the creditor's right to pursue the debt remains intact. The passage then clarifies a statement by al-Quduri: in his text, he noted 'except if they establish evidence' regarding the Sahibayn's position. This indicates that if there is conflicting testimony about solvency and insolvency, solvency testimony is given precedence because insolvency is the default state (norm) and insolvency witnesses merely affirm the norm, while solvency witnesses prove an exceptional state.

Key Terms
افلاس (iflas): The state of being bankrupt, having no sufficient wealth to pay debts.
صاحبین (Sahibayn): Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, the two main disciples of Imam Abu Hanifa.
قضاء بالافلاس (qada' bi al-iflas): A judge's formal verdict declaring someone bankrupt.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Abu Hanifa, why is a formal judgment of bankruptcy not valid?
A) Because the judge does not have the authority
B) Because wealth is transient and witnesses cannot be certain
C) Because the Quran prohibits declaring bankruptcy
D) Because only the creditors can declare bankruptcy

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, the relied-upon position is that a judge's declaration of bankruptcy is valid, granting the debtor a grace period, though Imam Abu Hanifa disagreed on epistemological grounds.

The Ruling on Mulāzamah: Creditor's Conduct with the Bankrupt Debtor
Ruling on the creditor's conduct when the debtor is inside his house

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on mulāzamah (accompaniment) for debt recovery and apply the conditions that determine its permissibility or prohibition.

Core Idea
A creditor cannot enter the debtor's house; mulāzamah is allowed unless it causes harm or involves unlawful seclusion.

This passage clarifies how a creditor may lawfully pursue a bankrupt debtor. It balances the creditor's right to recover debt with the debtor's right to privacy and protection from harm. The ruling introduces the concept of mulāzamah (accompaniment) and its limits, including a special rule for female debtors to avoid khalwa (seclusion).

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is the ruling on the creditor entering the debtor's house? State whether it is permitted or prohibited.
The passage begins with a clear prohibition: the creditor cannot enter.
2
Identify the default rule for accompaniment
When both the debtor and creditor choose between imprisonment and mulāzamah, who has the right to choose? And what is the condition for the judge to intervene?
The creditor's choice prevails unless mulāzamah causes clear harm.
3
Apply the rule to a female debtor
If the debtor is a woman, why can a male creditor not accompany her? What alternative is provided?
The prohibition of khalwa overrides the general permissibility of mulāzamah.
4
Summarize the overall rule
State the complete ruling in your own words, covering the key conditions and exceptions.
Ensure you mention the creditor's duty to wait outside, the harm exception, and the female debtor exception.

قرض خواہ مفلس کے گھر میں داخل نہیں ہو سکتے
ولو دخل فى داره لحاجته لا يتبعه بل يجلس على باب داره الى ان يخرج لان الانسان لابد ان يكون له موضع خلوة و لو اختار المطلوب الحبس والطالب الملازمة فالخيار الى الطالب لانه ابلغ في حصول المقصود لاختياره الاضيق عليه الا اذا علم القاضى ان يدخل عليه بالملازمة ضرر بين بان لا يمكنه من دخوله داره فحينئذ يحبسه دفعا للضرر عنه ولو كان الدين للرجل على المرأة لا يلازمها لما فيها من الخلوة بالاجنبية ولكن يبعث امرأة امينة تلازمها
ترجمه اگر مفلس مدیون کی ضرورت سے اپنے گھر میں داخل ہوا تو قرض خواہ اسکے پیچھے نہ جائے بلکہ گھر کے دروازو پر بیچا رہے یہاں تک کہ وہ باہر نکلے کیونکہ آدمی کیلئے کسی مقام خلوت کا ہونا ضروری ہے اور اگر مدیون نے اپنا محبوس رہنا اور دائن نے ساتھ رہنا اختیار کیا تو اختیار دائن کو ہو گا۔ کیونکہ اس کا مقصد حاصل ہونے میں یہ زیادہ قوی ہے۔ مدیون پر اس کے زیادہ تنگ بات اختیار کرنے میں وجہ سے مگر جبکہ قاضی کو معلوم ہو کہ ساتھ لگے رہنے میں مدیون کا کھلا ضر ر ہے بایں طور کہ وہ اس کو گھر میں نہ جانے دے تو اس صورت میں اس کو قید خانہ میں رکھے اس سے ضرر دور کرنے کیلئے اور اگر مرد کا قرض عورت پر ہو تو اس کے ساتھ نہر ہے۔ کیونکہ اس میں اجنبیہ کے ساتھ تنہائی ا زم آتی ہے۔ ہاں کسی امینہ عورت کو بھیج دے جو اس کے ساتھ رہے۔
Creditors cannot enter the bankrupt's house. If (the bankrupt) enters his house due to need, the creditor does not follow him, but sits at the door of his house until he comes out, because a person must have a place of solitude. And if the debtor chooses imprisonment and the creditor chooses accompaniment (talaazum), then the choice is for the creditor, because it is more effective in achieving the objective, due to his choosing the more constricting option for him. Except when the judge knows that accompaniment would cause clear harm, such that it prevents him from entering his house; then he imprisons him to repel the harm. And if the debt is owed by a man to a woman, he does not accompany her, because it involves seclusion with a non-mahram woman. Rather, he sends a trustworthy woman to accompany her.
Source pages p. 293

Commentary
This passage states the ruling on how a creditor may pursue a bankrupt debtor. The key rule is that the creditor cannot enter the debtor's house; if the debtor goes inside for a need, the creditor must wait outside at the door until the debtor exits, because everyone deserves a private space. The debtor may choose imprisonment over being shadowed, but the creditor may prefer accompaniment (following the debtor everywhere), and that choice is given to the creditor because it is more effective in recovering the debt. However, if the judge determines that this accompaniment would cause serious harm (e.g., the debtor cannot enter his own house), then the judge may imprison the debtor instead to prevent harm. Additionally, if the debt is on a woman, a male creditor cannot accompany her due to the prohibition of khalwa (seclusion) with a non-mahram. Instead, a trustworthy woman should be sent to accompany her.

Key Terms
ملازمة (mulāzamah): Accompaniment; a creditor following the debtor to ensure payment.
خلوة (khalwah): Seclusion between a man and a non-mahram woman without a third party.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) He can accompany her everywhere
B) He should send a trustworthy woman to accompany her
C) He can imprison her
D) He can enter her house

Key Takeaway
A creditor may not enter the debtor's home; mulāzamah is allowed unless it causes harm, and with a female debtor, a male creditor must appoint a trustworthy woman to avoid khalwa.

Seller's Status When Buyer Becomes Bankrupt: Hanafi vs. Shafi'i
Hanafi ruling that the seller is an ordinary creditor if buyer becomes bankrupt with the specific goods

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position that the seller is an ordinary creditor and explain the Hanafi response to the Shafi'i proof based on hadith and qiyas.

Core Idea
Bankruptcy does not give the seller a right to rescind; the seller is only an unsecured creditor.

When a buyer becomes bankrupt and the seller finds his specific goods in the buyer's possession, a critical dispute arises: should the seller get his property back, or must he share with all creditors? The Hanafi school rules that the seller has no special priority—he is treated like every other creditor. This lesson unpacks the Hanafi reasoning and its rebuttal of the contrary view, especially the Shafi'i position.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi ruling
Identify that the seller is equal to other creditors (uswah li al-ghuramā') and has no right to rescind the contract.
2
Recognize the opposing view
Note that Imām al-Shāfiʿī and others grant the seller a right of rescission (khiyār al-faskh) after the judge places the buyer under interdiction (hajr).
3
Examine the opponent's proof
Study the hadith cited by the Shāfiʿīs: 'Whoever finds his property with a bankrupt person, he is more entitled to it' and their qiyās on the seller's inability to deliver.
The hadith is mentioned in the source and its chain is critiqued.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
Understand why bankruptcy does not create a contractual right to rescission—the only obligation is the debt in the buyer's dhimmah. The sale is complete once possession passes, so the seller cannot unwind it.
The Hanafi argument is based on qiyās and the weakness of the opposing hadith.
5
Identify the practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf)
Contrast the outcomes: Hanafi – seller shares proportionally with all creditors; Shāfiʿī – seller reclaims the specific property entirely.

حجر (hajr): Legal interdiction; the judge places the buyer under restriction to prevent him from disposing of assets.

Liability of the Permitted Slave and Non-Temporal Permission
Ruling with proof via talil (reasoning)

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state that the master is not liable for the permitted slave's debts and explain why a time-limited permission becomes indefinite until restriction.

Core Idea
Permission is isqat; isqat cannot be time-bound, and the master retains hajr authority.

This passage from Al-Hidayah establishes two key rulings for the permitted slave (al-ma'dhun). First, the master bears no liability for the slave's transactions. Second, permission is a relinquishment (isqat) of the right to prevent, and an isqat cannot be confined to a period. These rulings rest on the nature of the slave's own legal capacity and the character of isqat.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core ruling
The text states that the slave's liability does not transfer to the master. Note the reason: the slave transacts with his own capacity.
2
Understand the nature of permission
Permission is treated as an isqat, not a mere ibaha. This affects its revocability and temporal limitations.
3
Apply the rule to a time-limited permission
If the master says 'I permit you for a day,' it is effectively permanent because isqat cannot be time-bound. The only way to end it is by a formal restriction (hajr).
4
Resolve the apparent contradiction
Objection: if isqat drops the right to prevent, how can the master later restrict? Answer: The master’s authority to restrict (hajr) comes from slavery itself, not from the dropped right. The isqat only covers the right to prevent during the permission period.
This is a key distinction in Hanafi usul between the right dropped (isqat) and the inherent authority (wilayah) derived from slavery.
5
Clarify the role of definition in argument
The objection that 'definition does not allow argumentation' is answered by distinguishing tasawwurat (concepts) from tasdiqat (verifications). The argument is about the ruling derived from the definition, not the definition itself.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولهذا لا يرجع بما لحقه من العهدة على المولى ولهذا لا يقبل التوقيت حتى لو اذن لعبده يوما كان ماذونا ابدا حتى يحجر عليه لان الاسقاطات لا تتوقت

ترجمہ اس لئے جو ذمہ داری اس کو لاحق ہو وہ آقا پر عائد نہیں ہوتی اور اسی لئے اجازت توقیت کو قبول نہیں کرتی ۔ یہاں تک کہ اگر غلام کو ایک روز کی اجازت دی تو وہ ہمیشہ کے لئے مازون ہو جائے گا یہاں تک کہ اس کو مجبور کر دے۔ کیونکہ اسقاطات موقت نہیں ہوتے

تشریح قوله ولهذا لا يرجع ... الخ يقول سابق و العبد بعد ذالك يتصرف لنفسه باهليته" کی طرف راجی ہے۔ یعنی اجازت کے بعد غلام چونکہ اپنی لیاقت سے تصرف کرتا ہے اس لئے جو ذمہ داری اس کو لاحق ہو وہ آقا یر عائد نہیں ہوتی یعنی آقا اس کا ضامن نہیں ہوتا۔ اور اسی لئے کہ اذن ہمارے نزدیک اسقاط حق ہے۔ اجازت کسی وقت تک می رود نہیں ہوتی۔ حتی کہ اگر غلام کو ایک روز کے لئے تجارت کی اجازت دی تو وہ ہمیشہ کے لئے مازون ہو جائے گا یہاں تک کہ آقا اس کو مجبور کرے وجہ یہ ہے کہ اسقاطات کسی وقت کے ساتھ موقت نہیں ہوتے۔

سوال اگر اذن مذکور اسقاط ہو تو اسقاط کے بعد آقا کے لئے ولایت حجر نہیں ہونی چاہیے۔ کیونکہ وہ اپنا حق ساقط کر چکا اور جو چیز ساقط ہو جائے وہ عود نہیں کرتی۔ حالانکہ آقا اس کے بعد بھی حجر کر سکتا ہے۔ جواب ... ولایت حجر بقاء رقیت کے اعتبار سے ہے۔ پس اجازت کے بعد پھر حجر کرنے میں اس حق کے اسقاط سے رکنا ہے جو اس کو بلحاظ مستقبل حاصل ہے اس اعتبار سے نہیں ہے کہ ساقط عود کر آیا۔

سوالف حجر اور اسقاط حق تو حیز تعریف میں مذکور ہے۔ حيث قال : وفی الشرع فك الحجر و اسقاط الحق‘ پھر اس پر استدلال کیسے درست ہوا۔ جواب استدلال مذکور نفس تعریف پر نہیں ہے یہاں تک کہ یہ اعتراض وارد ہو کہ تعریف استدلال کو قبول نہیں کرتی ۔ کیونکہ تعریف از قبیل تصورات ہے اور تصور کا اکتساب تصدیق سے نہیں ہوتا بلکہ استدلال حکم ضمنی پر ہے۔ "كان يقال هذا التعريف صحيح هذا التعريف مطابق لاصولنا اور ظاہر ہے کہ یہ احکام تصدیقات ہیں جن پر استدلال کیا جاسکتا ہے۔
Because of this, he does not have recourse against the master for any liability that befell him, and because of this, permission does not accept temporal limitation, so that if he permits his slave for a day, he is permitted forever until he places a restriction on him, because relinquishments are not time-bound. [Translation of matn only] Translation: Because of this, the liability that befalls him does not revert to the master. And because of this, permission does not accept temporal limitation. Even if he granted permission to the slave for one day, he will be permitted forever until (the master) constrains him. Because relinquishments are not temporal. Explanation: The saying 'And because of this he does not have recourse...' relates back to the earlier statement that after that the slave transacts with his own capacity. That is, after permission, since the slave transacts with his own capacity, any liability that befalls him does not revert to the master; i.e., the master is not his guarantor. And because of this, permission in our view is an isqat of a right. Permission cannot be temporally bound. Even if the master gave permission for one day, the slave is permanently permitted until the master constrains him. The reason is that relinquishments are not temporal. Question: If the aforementioned permission is an isqat, then after isqat, the master should not have the authority to restrict (hajr), because he has already dropped his right, and what has dropped cannot return. Yet the master can still restrict. Answer: The authority to restrict is due to the persistence of slavery. Thus after permission, the restriction is from the right he possesses for the future; it is not that the dropped right returns. Another question: The restriction and isqat are mentioned in the definition: 'In the Shari'ah, it is the removal of restriction and the relinquishment of a right.' So how is the istidlal correct? Answer: The mentioned istidlal is not on the definition itself, so that the objection that definition does not admit argumentation would be valid. Because definition is from the category of concepts (tasawwurat) and concept is not acquired from verification (tasdiq). Rather, the istidlal is on the implicit ruling. It is said: 'This definition is correct, this definition is in accordance with our principles.' And obviously these are rulings of verification which can be argued upon.
Source pages p. 298

Commentary
This passage opens with the fundamental rule that when a master permits his slave to trade, any debts or liabilities arising from the slave's transactions fall solely on the slave, not the master. The reason is that the slave transacts with his own legal capacity (ahliyyah). The text then addresses the nature of permission: it is an isqat (relinquishment) of the master's right to prevent the slave from trading. Since isqat cannot be limited to a specific period, a time-limited permission (e.g., for one day) effectively gives permanent permission until the master explicitly revokes it. The commentary then addresses a significant objection: if permission is an isqat, how can the master later place a restriction (hajr) on the slave? The answer is that the master's authority to restrict remains due to the ongoing slavery ('ubudiyyah). The isqat only pertains to the period before restriction; it does not remove the master's right to restrict for the future. A second objection concerns the definition of hajr and isqat. The response clarifies that the argument is not about the definition itself (which is a conceptual matter) but about the implicit ruling derived from it. This discussion is characteristic of Hanafi usul al-fiqh, distinguishing between tasawwurat (concepts) and tasdiqat (verifications).

Key Terms
إسقاط (isqat): The relinquishment or dropping of a right; here specifically the master's right to prevent the slave from trading.
إذن (idhn): Permission granted by the master to his slave to engage in trade; considered an isqat of the right to prevent.
توقيت (taqit): The act of limiting something to a specific time; here, it is rejected because isqat cannot be bound by time.
حجر (hajr): The act of restricting the slave from trading; the master's authority to place a restriction.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

Permission Established by Implication: Silence as Consent – Hanafi vs Zufar/Shafi'i
Ruling that silence constitutes permission, with Hanafi vs Zufar/Shafi'i dispute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school considers a master's silence as permission for his slave to trade, and evaluate the counter-argument by Zufar and al-Shafi'i.

Core Idea
Silence in a context of expression is consent: a master who sees his slave trading and does not object grants permission by implication.

This passage addresses a practical scenario: a master observes his slave engaging in buying and selling but remains silent. Does this silence constitute permission to trade, or must permission be explicitly stated? The Hanafi school, based on the maxim 'al-sukut fi ma'rid al-bayan bayan' (silence in a context requiring explanation is a statement), holds that silence indeed implies consent. This ruling is contested by Zufar (a Hanafi jurist) and al-Shafi'i, who argue that silence could stem from other motives such as anger. Understanding this dispute illuminates how Hanafi legal methodology balances public interest (preventing harm) against individual rights.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the precise ruling: the master's silence when seeing his slave trade makes the slave a permitted trader (ma'dhun). This is established by implication (dalalah al-hal), not explicit speech.
The ruling applies regardless of whether the slave sells the master's property or a stranger's, and whether the sale is valid or corrupt.
2
Identify the opposing view and its basis
Zufar and al-Shafi'i disagree. They argue that silence does not imply permission because the master is not obligated to prevent hypothetical harm, and silence could be due to anger or displeasure rather than consent.
This opposition is part of a legitimate ikhtilaf within and across schools.
3
Analyze the Hanafi proof (dalil)
The Hanafi proof is based on the prevention of harm (darar): the public will assume the slave is permitted and transact with him, suffering loss if he is not. The master's natural reaction, if displeased, would be to forbid. Thus, his silence is legally interpreted as consent.
Key maxim: al-sukut fi ma'rid al-bayan bayan.
4
Evaluate the Hanafi response to the opposing argument
The Hanafi response counters that the public's interpretation is decisive; after debts accrue, the master's excuse of anger will not be accepted. Silence in a context that demands explanation is treated as a statement.
This shows how Hanafi usul prioritizes objective circumstances over subjective intent.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
Per the Hanafi position, the slave is deemed permitted and all his transactions are binding on him and the master. Per Zufar and al-Shafi'i, the slave is not permitted unless explicitly authorized, and third parties bear the risk.
This difference affects the validity of transactions and liability for debts.

Source Text

قوله ثم الاذن كما يثبت ..... الخ تصرف کی اجازت جس طرح نطق صریح سے ثابت ہوتی ہے۔ اسی طرح دلالت حال سے بھی ثابت ہو جاتی ہے۔ مثلاً آقا نے اپنے غلام کو خرید و فروخت کرتے دیکھا اور خاموش رہا تو یہ اسکی طرف سے اجازت ہے غلام ماذون في التجارہ ہو جائے گا آقا کا مال بیچا ہو یا کسی اجنبی کا بی صحیح کی ہو یا فاسد۔ عنایہ، زیلعی متقی ، اور شرنبلالیہ وغیرہ میں بھی ایسا ہی ہے وجہ یہ ہے کہ جو شخص بھی اس غلام کو ایسا کرتے دیکھے گا وہ اسکو ماذون ہی سمجھے گا اور اس کے ساتھ معاملہ کرے گا اب اگر وہ ماذون نہ ہو تو ضرر لاحق ہو گا اور آقا اگر اس پر راضی نہ ہوتا تو وہ اس کو منع کر دیتا تا کہ لوگوں کا ضرر دور ہو۔

امام زفر اور ائمہ ثلاثہ کے یہاں سکوت مذکور سے اجازت ثابت نہیں ہوتی وہ یہ فرماتے ہیں کہ لوگوں کا خیالی ضرر دور کرنا آقا پر واجب نہیں رہا۔ اس کا خاموش رہنا ہو ممکن ہے وہ اس وجہ سے ہو کہ اس کو اپنے غلام کا فعل بلا اجازت ہونے کی وجہ سے ناگوار ہوا ہے تو وہ غصہ سے خاموش ہو گیا۔ جواب یہ ہے کہ لوگ اس کے سکوت کو اجازت ہی سمجھیں گے۔ کیونکہ یہ بیان کا موقع ہے اور اس کے بعد جب غلام پر قرضہ چڑھ جائے گا تو وہ لوگ اس کی گردن سے وصول کر لیں گے اور اس وقت آقا کا یہ عذر کارگر نہ ہوگا کہ میں تو غصہ کی وجہ سے خاموش رہا تھا۔
Then permission, just as it is established explicitly, is also established by implication, as when he sees his slave buying and selling and remains silent, he becomes a permitted slave according to us, contrary to Zufar and al-Shafi'i. There is no distinction between selling property owned by the master or by a stranger, whether with permission or without permission, whether a valid sale or a corrupt (fasid) sale, because everyone who sees him thinks he is permitted for it and thus contracts with him, and he would suffer harm if he were not permitted. And if the master were not pleased with it, he would have prevented him to ward off harm from them. [matn]
Commentary: 'Then permission...' Permission to transact is established by explicit speech just as it is established by the implication of the situation. For example, the master saw his slave buying and selling and remained silent, this is permission from him; the slave becomes permitted in trade, whether he sold the master's property or a stranger's, whether the sale was valid or invalid. In 'Inayah, Zayla'i, Muttaqi, and Shurunbulaliyya it is similar. The reason is that anyone who sees this slave doing so will consider him permitted and will transact with him; now if he were not permitted, harm would befall, and if the master were not pleased, he would have forbidden him to remove harm from the people.

According to Imam Zufar and the three Imams, silence does not establish permission. They say: The master is not obliged to remove imaginary harm from people. His silence could be because he was displeased with his slave's act without permission and remained silent out of anger. The answer is that people will consider his silence as permission, because this is a place of expression; and later when the slave incurs debts, they will collect from his neck, and the master's excuse that he was silent out of anger will not be accepted.
Source pages p. 298

Commentary
This passage establishes that permission to trade can be inferred from the master's silence when he sees his slave engaged in commerce. The Hanafi school holds that if the master is aware and does not object, his silence constitutes consent because the natural reaction of a displeased master is to forbid. The principle is the prevention of harm (darar) — if the slave were not permitted, people would suffer losses in their transactions. Zufar and al-Shafi'i argue that the master is not obligated to prevent imaginary harm and silence could be due to anger or other reasons. The Hanafi response counters that the public will interpret silence as permission, and after the slave incurs debts, the master's excuse will not be accepted. This is based on the legal maxim that silence in a context requiring explanation is considered a statement (al-sukut fi ma'rid al-bayan bayan). The commentary also notes that there is no distinction regarding what type of property is sold or whether the sale is valid or invalid, emphasizing that the appearance of being a trader is enough to bind the master's consent.

Key Terms
سكوت (sukut): Silence; the master's lack of objection upon seeing the slave trade, which is interpreted as consent in this context.
دلالة (dalalah): Implication; here, the circumstantial evidence that indicates permission.
ضرر (darar): Harm; the basis for the Hanafi ruling, as silence prevents harm to the public who transact with the slave.
مأذون (ma'dhun): The permitted slave; a slave who has been given permission by his master to trade.

D) Because the slave has full legal capacity.

Key Takeaway
Silence in a context where speech is expected is legally treated as consent, a key Hanafi principle rooted in preventing harm to third parties.

Scope of General Permission for Trade
Defines the scope of a general trade permission.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the scope of a general trade permission for a slave and list all authorized transactions.

Core Idea
A general trade permission authorizes all commercial transactions because 'tijara' is a generic noun.

This passage from Al-Hidayah explains the effect of an unrestricted permission to trade given to a slave. The key is the linguistic principle that the word 'tijara' (trade) with the definite article denotes generality, covering every form of commercial exchange. Understanding this ruling helps you apply the same principle to similar permissions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its condition
The master gives the slave a general, unrestricted permission to trade. The condition for the ruling is that the permission is not limited to a specific type of trade.
The permission must be general, e.g., 'I have permitted you to trade' without qualification.
2
Recognize the key term 'tijara'
The word 'tijara' (تجارة) is a generic noun (ism jins) and when used with the definite article, it indicates generality (umum) in Hanafi usul.
This linguistic classification drives the legal conclusion.
3
Apply the principle of umum
Since 'tijara' is general, it includes all instances of trade-related acts. The slave is authorized to engage in any lawful commercial activity.
The principle is: 'Al-amm yashmalu jami'a afradihi' (the general includes all its individuals).
4
List the authorized transactions
The text explicitly mentions buying, selling, agency, pledge, hire, mudarabah, admitting debts, deposits, and even claims of usurpation. All are valid.
The list is illustrative, not exhaustive, because 'tijara' covers all necessities of trade.
5
Note the consequence of restriction
If the master restricts the permission to a specific type of trade (e.g., 'only in cloth'), the slave is limited to that type.
This contrast highlights the importance of the general wording.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال واذا اذن المولى لعبده في التجارة اذنا عاما جاز تصرفه في سائر التجارات ومعنى هذه المسألة ان يقول له اذنت لك فى التجارة ولا يقيده ووجهه ان التجارة اسم عام يتناول الجنس فيبيع ويشترى ما بدا له من انواع الاعيان لانه اصل التجارة

ترجمہ جب اجازت دے دی آقا نے اپنے غلام کو تجارت کی عام اجازت تو جائز ہوگا اس کا تصرف جملہ تجارات میں اور اس مسئلہ کے معنی یہ ہیں کہ آقا غلام سے یہ کہے کہ میں نے تجھے تجارت کی اجازت دے دی اور اس کو مقید نہ کرے اور وجہ اس کی یہ ہے کہ تجارت اسم عام ہے جو جنس تجارت کو شامل ہے۔ پس غلام بیچے اور خرید لے جو اس کا جی چاہے انواع اشیاء میں سے کیونکہ یہی اصل تجارت ہے۔

تشریح قوله واذا اذن .. الخ اگر آقا نے اپنے غلام کو عام اجازت دی کسی معین چیز یا خاص قسم کی تجارت کے ساتھ مقید نہیں کیا۔ مثلاً یوں کہا کہ میں نے تجھے تجارت کی اجازت دے دی۔ تو غلام ہر قسم کی تجارت کا مجاز ہوگا یعنی اس کے لئے خرید نا ، فروخت کرنا ، وکیل بنانا، رہن لینا، اجرت پر لینا ، مضاربت کرنا ، دین یا ودیعت یا غصب کا اقرار کرنا ۔ غرض تمام تصرفات جائز ہیں ۔ کیونکہ آقا کے قول ۔ اذنت لک فی التجارة " میں لفظ تجارت اسم جنس محلی بالسلام ہے تو یہ عام ہوئی ۔ لہذ اجملہ انواع تجارت ولوازم تجارت کو شامل ہوگی۔
He said: When the master gives his slave a general permission for trade, his transactions in all types of trade are permissible. The meaning of this issue is that he says to him: 'I have permitted you to trade' and does not restrict it. Its reasoning is that 'trade' is a generic noun covering the genus. So he buys and sells whatever types of goods he wishes because that is the origin of trade.

Translation: When the master gives his slave a general permission for trade, his transactions in all types of trade are permissible. The meaning of this issue is that the master says to the slave: 'I have permitted you to trade' and does not restrict it. Its reasoning is that 'trade' is a generic noun covering the genus of trade. So the slave buys and sells whatever he wishes from among types of goods because that is the essence of trade.

Explanation: The saying 'And when he permits...' If the master gave his slave a general permission, not restricted to a particular item or specific type of trade, for example he said: 'I have permitted you to trade', then the slave is authorized for every type of trade. That is, buying, selling, appointing an agent, taking a pledge, hiring, mudarabah, admitting a debt, deposit, or usurpation. In short, all transactions are permissible. Because in the master's statement 'I have permitted you to trade', the word 'trade' is a generic noun with the definite article, so it is general. Therefore, it includes all types of trade and its necessities.
Source pages p. 299

Commentary
This passage clarifies that when a master gives his slave a general, unrestricted permission to engage in trade, the slave is authorized to conduct all forms of commercial transactions. The word 'tijara' (trade) in Arabic is a generic noun (ism jins) that, when used with the definite article, indicates generality (umum). Therefore, it encompasses buying, selling, agency, mortgage, hire, mudarabah, and even admissions of debt or liability. The key is that the permission is not confined to a specific commodity or trade. The slave may deal in any lawful goods. This ruling underscores the Hanafi principle that words are understood according to their customary usage and that a general permission grants wide authority unless the master explicitly restricts it.

Key Terms
تجارة (tijara): A generic term covering all types of commercial exchange.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) All commercial transactions including agency, pledge, hire, and mudarabah.
D) Only transactions that do not involve admitting debts.

Key Takeaway
A general trade permission authorizes all commercial transactions because the word 'tijara' encompasses all types of trade.

Sale with Excessive Deception by a Permitted Slave
Intra-Hanafi dispute on ghabn fahish

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding ghabn fahish in a permitted slave's sale and explain the thamarah al-khilaf.

Core Idea
Excessive deception by a permitted slave: a valid sale to Abu Hanifa, a gift to the Sahibayn.

The Hanafi school recognizes that some deception in trade is unavoidable, but what happens when a permitted slave sells or buys at a price far from market value? The imams differ on whether such a transaction exceeds the scope of the slave's permission. This passage records the dispute and the reasoning behind each view.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position (Abu Hanifa)
Identify Abu Hanifa's ruling: the sale with ghabn fahish is valid. Note his proof that the slave transacts with his own capacity, like a free person.
2
Identify the opposing view (Sahibayn)
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad hold that such a sale is invalid (fasid) because it is akin to a gift (tabarru'), which is not covered by the permission to trade.
3
Follow the Sahibayn's proof
Understand their qiyas: just as a sick person's excessive transaction is limited to one-third of his wealth, a slave's excessive transaction is beyond the permission.
They analogize to the rule of the marid (sick person).
4
Analyse Abu Hanifa's proof
Abu Hanifa focuses on the slave's ahliyyah (legal capacity) for trade, treating the transaction as a regular trade regardless of the price deviation.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: under Abu Hanifa the sale is binding; under the Sahibayn it is void unless the master ratifies it.
The text does not specify which view is mufta bihi, but later fatwa often prefers the Sahibayn's caution.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال ولو باع او اشترى بالغبن اليسير فهو جائز لتعذر الاحتراز عنه وكذا بالفاحش عند ابي حنيفة خلافا لهما هما يقولان ان البيع بالفاحش منه بمنزلة التبرع حتى اعتبر المريض من ثلث ماله فلا ينتظمه الاذن كالهبة وله انه تجارة والعبد متصرف باهلية نفسه فصار كالحر وعلى هذا الخلاف الصبى الماذون ولو
He said: If he sells or buys with slight deception (ghabn yasir), it is valid because it is impossible to avoid. Similarly, with excessive deception (ghabn fahish) according to Abu Hanifa, contrary to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad). They say: The sale with excessive deception is like a gift (tabarru'), so that the sick person's (transaction) is considered from one-third of his wealth, so permission does not cover it like a gift. And (Abu Hanifa's) proof is that it is trade, and the slave transacts with his own capacity, so he becomes like a free person. And upon this difference is the permitted minor (al-sabi al-ma'dhun) and...
Source pages p. 299

Commentary
This passage addresses whether a permitted slave's sale with deception (ghabn) is valid. Ghabn is selling or buying at a price that deviates from the fair market value. Slight deception (ghabn yasir) is unavoidable in trade, so it is valid by consensus. The dispute concerns excessive deception (ghabn fahish). Abu Hanifa rules that such a sale is still valid because the slave is acting with his own legal capacity, just like a free person would be bound. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) analogize it to a gift (tabarru'), which is not covered by the permission to trade. They argue that if a sick person makes such a sale, it is considered from one-third of his estate, indicating it is beyond the scope of normal trade. The same ruling applies to a permitted minor (sabi ma'dhun). The text seems to cut off, likely the continuation would discuss the measure of fahish and perhaps the ruling of the Sahibayn being the preferred view in later fatwa.

Key Terms
غبن (ghabn): Deception in the price; selling or buying at a non-market price.
يسير (yasir): Slight; unavoidable deception that is tolerated.
فاحش (fahish): Excessive; deception that is akin to a gift because of large deviation.
تبرع (tabarru'): Gratuitous act; gift; not covered by permission to trade according to the Sahibayn.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because the slave is acting as an agent for the master.
B) Because it is still a form of trade and the slave has legal capacity.
C) Because the master's permission implicitly covers all types of deception.
D) Because the buyer is not harmed.

Key Takeaway
The validity of a sale with excessive deception by a permitted slave depends on whether it is seen as trade (Abu Hanifa) or as a gift (Sahibayn), leading to different outcomes for the buyer and master.

The Ma'dhun Slave's Debts and the Master's Right to Profit
Detailed commentary on three sub-rules

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state and explain the three rulings regarding a ma'dhun slave's debts and the master's limited right to profit, applying the reasoning from the commentary.

Core Idea
Debts of a ma'dhun slave attach to his earnings; the master may only take the equivalent of normal profit, not the surplus.

This lesson unpacks a detailed commentary on three interconnected rulings concerning a ma'dhun slave—a slave permitted to trade. The commentary explains why the slave cannot be resold if the first sale fails to cover debts, how debts attach to earnings and gifts, and the master's limited right to take profit. Understanding this reasoning is essential for grasping the Hanafi approach to protecting creditors' rights while preserving the master's incentive.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three sub-rules
The commentary addresses three distinct issues: (1) no second sale of the slave if debts remain, (2) debts attach to the slave's earnings, and (3) the master may take only the equivalent normal profit. Read the commentary to extract these three rulings.
2
Analyze the first rule: No second sale
The commentary explains that selling the slave again would deter buyers and harm the first purchaser, who did not consent to the trade debts. Contrast this with the master, who accepted the risk of trade by giving permission.
Focus on the distinction between the purchaser and the master.
3
Analyze the second rule: Debt attachment to earnings
Debts attach to all earnings and gifts still in the slave's possession, whether acquired before or after the debt. The master cannot claim ownership until the slave's needs (including debt repayment) are satisfied.
Note the exception: property removed by the master before the debt is free.
4
Analyze the third rule: Master's right to profit
The master may take an amount equal to the normal profit of a similar slave (ghullat mithl). Any excess must go to creditors. This prevents the master from restricting the slave's work and ensures creditors' rights.
This is a balance between the master's interest and the creditors' rights.
5
Apply the reasoning to a scenario
Consider a scenario: a ma'dhun slave earns 100 dirhams, the normal profit is 60 dirhams, and he has debts of 80 dirhams. Determine how much the master can take and how much goes to creditors.
Use the rule: master takes 60 dirhams, 40 dirhams goes to creditors (but creditors still have 40 dirhams remaining debt).
